## 3.4 Relations between the models 

In the previous section, we defined the level-1 lifted MFMDP and we proved some of its main properties. We now reconnect this model with the original agent level MFC problem and derive properties of the latter from what we just found out about the lifted model CHAPTER 3. A GENERAL MATHEMATICAL MFRL MODEL WITH COMMON NOISE 57 

3.4.1 Relations between MFC closed-loop policies and MFMDP policies 

We start by highlighting that, intuitively, a closed-loop policy for the MFC can be viewed as sampled from a policy for the MFMDP by picking the common randomness. The following definition formalizes this idea. 

Definition 3.12. Let ŌÄ P ő†CL and ¬Į ŌÄ P ¬Įő†. We say that they correspond to each other 

if for each őľ P ¬ĮS and n ńõ 0, ¬Į ŌÄnpőľq P Pp ¬ĮAq is equal to the push forward of PŌĎ0 by the map: őė0 Q őł0 √ě √Ď őľ ňÜb ŌÄnp¬® , őľ, őł 0q P ¬ĮA, 

where ňÜb denotes the product of a measure and a kernel. Note that if ŌÄ and ¬Į ŌÄ correspond to each other and if ŌÄ is stationary, then so is ¬Į ŌÄ.Conversely, if ŌÄ and ¬Į ŌÄ correspond to each other and if ¬Į ŌÄ is stationary, then ŌÄ can be chosen to be stationary. We will provide a specific construction in equation (3.25) below. The main result of this section is the following. 

Theorem 3.2. Assume that the continuity assumption (Assumption 5) holds. Then for ev-ery őľ P PpSq, for every ŌÄ P ő†CL , there exists ¬ĮŌÄ P ¬Įő† such that JŌÄpőľq ‚Äú ¬ĮJ ¬ĮŌÄpőľq. Conversely, for every őľ P PpSq, for every ¬ĮŌÄ P ¬Įő†, there exists ŌÄ P ő†CL such that JŌÄpőľq ‚Äú ¬ĮJ ¬ĮŌÄpőľq.In particular, for stationary policies, we have: for every őľ P PpSq, for every stationary 

ŌÄ P ő†CL , there exists a level-1 mixed strategy function ¬ĮŌÄ P ¬Įő† such that JŌÄpőľq ‚Äú ¬ĮJ ¬ĮŌÄpőľq, and conversely, for every őľ P PpSq, for every stationary policy ¬ĮŌÄ P ¬Įő† with strategy function ¬ĮŌÄ,there exists a stationary ŌÄ P ő†CL such that JŌÄpőľq ‚Äú ¬ĮJ ¬ĮŌÄpőľq.

## 3.5 Mean-Field Q-Function 

In preparation for our discussion of learning in the third part of the monograph, and in line with our analysis of the first theoretical model of MFC, we introduce the state-action value function, for the lifted MFMDP. Using standard arguments, we derive its first theoretical properties. This will pave the way to the algorithmic search for approximations of the solution of the MFC problem in a model-free setting, i.e. assuming the model is unknown. 

3.5.1 State-action value function 

In order to take full advantage of the strongest results proven so far, from now on we assume that both assumptions 5 and 6 hold. ## 3.6 Notes and Complements 

MFMDPs have been studied at least since the work of Gast et al. [70, 71] who studied the convergence of a finite-agent MDP towards a MFMDP. More recently, Gu et al. studied in [79, 77] dynamic programming for the state-action value function arising in MFC problems without common noise, and they proposed RL methods for this setting. Following the posting on arxiv of the original version of our paper [40], Motte and Pham tackled in [119] the open loop case in the presence of common noise, in a model very similar to the model considered in this chapter. They proved a propagation of chaos result in the spirit of Proposition 2.3 of Chapter 2, a DPP for the MFMDP, as well as the equality of optimal value functions for open-loop and closed-loop policies in their model. In this chapter, we proposed a finer model by paying close attention to all the sources of randomization, and by relying on fine measure theoretical arguments. Comparing to [119], while they prove equality of the optimal value functions without the central randomization, the latter allows us to prove the identity of the value functions, policy by policy, even before taking the optimum values, allowing for a deeper understanding of the agent level MFC, as did for example in Corollary 3.1. Note that when the state space S is discrete, assuming continuity of F p¬® , ¬®, ¬®, e, e 0q implies that the dependence on the mean-field term is basically constant when the other inputs are fixed. This is very much in line with the fact that many MFG and MFC models appearing in the literature do not have mean-field interactions in the dynamics of the states, the interactions appearing explicitly only in the cost functions. See for example the original models in [103] as well as the applications to Bertrand and Cournot competitions [81, 41], and the models of crowd motion [3] or flocking [122, 13]. To cover models with interactions in the dynamics of the states in a finite state space setting, one should use, instead of the assumptions mentioned in Remark 3.7, other assumptions that are sufficient to guarantee Assumption 5. In particular, it is expected that the idiosyncratic noise has a regularizing effect that helps ensuring continuity of ¬ĮF even when F is discontinuous. For instance [119, Proposition 2.1] does not require continuity of F but only some kind of almost everywhere continuity. Chapter 4 

# A Linear Quadratic Mean Field Model with Common Noise 

Abstract. In this chapter, we provide a complete theoretical analysis of the Linear Quadratic (LQ) model of Mean Field Control (MFC) with common noise which was introduced in Section 2.6 of Chapter 2. We shall use the notation LQMFC to denote such a model. We characterize optimal policies and prove their existence, preparing for the numerical analysis and RL implementations of Chapter 6. 

## 4.1 Introduction 

The thrust of the chapter is to properly introduce the notations and the definitions of the linear-quadratic (LQ) mean field control (MFC), or LQMFC, stochastic optimization prob-lem which was introduced in broad strokes in Chapter 2. After arguing that the model appears naturally as the limit of a large multi agent model with a common noise, we prove that the optimal admissible controls exist, are unique, and linear in the state and its condi-tional mean processes. We formulate and prove the necessary and the sufficient conditions for a discrete-time form of the Pontryagin maximum principle for our LQMFC model. This result implies existence and uniqueness of the optimal control process. Incidentally, this provides a crucial link between the MFC problem and learning by a finite population of agents, as it gives an approximately optimal control for the problem with a finite number of learners. 

## 4.2 Models 

We work with the model introduced at the beginning of Section 2.6 of Chapter 2 and we repeat the meaning of the notations only when necessary. Because the original probability space pő©, F, Pq is assumed to be rich enough, to facilitate the analysis for LQ models, we can introduce two initial random variables, denoted by őĶ00 and őĶ0, called initial perturbations ,that are independent of each other and independent of the noise sequences őĶ ‚Äú p őĶnqnńõ1

They are not assumed to follow the mean-zero distributions of 67 CHAPTER 4. A LINEAR QUADRATIC MEAN FIELD MODEL WITH COMMON NOISE 68 the noise sequences. Also, they can be constant, i.e. random variables with a Dirac measure as distribution. This is the case when we want the LQ model to start with a deterministic initial condition. In this chapter, we assume that the initial perturbations őĶ0 and őĶ00, and all noise terms

Remark 4.2 . It is worth pointing out that our definition of the set of admissible controls 

Aad is slightly different than the one considered in [61] for an LQ model without mean-field interactions. Indeed, Remark 4.1 highlights that under our current assumptions, we have 

a P Aad √ī Jpaq ńÉ 8 . In addition, these authors focus on control coefficient K P R‚ĄďňÜd such that the spectral radius of A ¬ī BK satisfies ŌĀpA ¬ī BKq ńÉ 1. The set tK | ŌĀpA ¬ī BKq ńÉ 1u

Given that ŌĀpA ¬ī BKq ńŹ } A ¬ī BK} for any matrix K P R‚ĄďňÜd, our definition of admissibility is more restrictive than the one considered in [61]. As a consequence, a proper analysis of the admissibility of the optimal control is necessary. We will provide more details in Section 4.3.1.

The following theorem shows that we can construct an optimal control process a for the MFC problem (4.12) based on a solution of the FBDTSE system (4.44).

## 4.4 Notes and Complements 

Discrete-time LQ mean field control problems without common noise are treated in [58, 121], where the analysis leads to decoupled Riccati equations. Continuous-time LQ mean field control problems and related McKean‚ÄďVlasov control formulations are studied, e.g., in [161, 86, 74]; see also [34, Chapter 6] for a systematic treatment of the continuous-time theory. Classical LQR problems without mean-field interactions have also been studied from the reinforcement learning perspective, in particular for learning linear feedback gains; see [133, 61, 158]. The policy-gradient analysis for the LQ mean field model considered here is developed in Chapter 6. Part III 

# Numerical Implementations 

> 87

# Chapter 5 

# Implementation of the First Abstract Model 

Abstract. This chapter presents numerical methods for solving the Mean Field Markov Decision Processes (MFMDPs) introduced in Chapter 3. We first describe a tabular Q-learning algorithm that uses simplex discretization to handle the measure-valued state space. A proof of convergence is provided for this approach. To improve scalability, we then discuss an adaptation of the Deep Deterministic Policy Gradient (DDPG) algorithm. This method leverages neural network approximations to handle continuous state and action spaces, as motivated by the theoretical developments in the first part of this monograph. 

## 5.1 Introduction 

This chapter is the first of two dedicated to the numerical resolution of Mean Field Control (MFC) through reinforcement learning. It bridges the gap between the rigorous mathemat-ical models developed in Chapter 3 and their practical implementation. As discussed in Chapter 3, finding optimal policies for mean field Markov decision processes (MFMDPs) re-quires overcoming significant computational hurdles, primarily due to the potential infinite-dimensional nature of the lifted state space consisting of probability measures. We revisit the concept of the ‚Äúlifted‚ÄĚ (level-1) problem, where the central planner controls the flow of distributions, and contrast it with the level-0 dynamics of individual agents. To address this challenge in the general setting, we present two distinct approaches. First, we discuss a method based on space discretization, leading to a tabular version of the Q-learning algorithm. By projecting the mean-field distributions onto a finite grid (a discretized simplex), we can rigorously establish the convergence of the algorithm (The-orem 5.1). However, as we shall see, this approach suffers from the curse of dimensionality, making it impractical for problems where the level-0 state and action spaces are large. Second, in order to circumvent the limitations of discretizations, we introduce function approximation techniques, specifically adapting the Deep Deterministic Policy Gradient (DDPG) algorithm. This approach operates directly on continuous state and action spaces by relying on artificial neural networks to approximate the Q-function (the critic) and the policy (the actor). While theoretical convergence guarantees for deep RL in the mean field 88 CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE FIRST ABSTRACT MODEL 89 setting remain an active area of research, the numerical experiments reported in Section 5.4 demonstrate the practical efficacy of this approach for models ranging from cybersecurity to distribution planning. By exploring both a theoretically grounded discrete method and a scalable continu-ous method, this chapter equips the reader with the tools necessary to deploy Mean Field Reinforcement Learning algorithms in diverse and complex environments. These methods prepare the way for Chapter 6, where we specifically focus on Linear-Quadratic MFC prob-lems for which we develop a more refined policy gradient analysis and provide stronger convergence guarantees. 

## 5.2 Background on Reinforcement Learning Methods 

Before delving into the specific algorithms tailored to MFC, we briefly review the core concepts of Reinforcement Learning (RL) that underpin the methodologies presented in this and the subsequent chapter. 

5.2.1 The Reinforcement Learning paradigm 

The core objective of RL is to solve an optimal control problem formulated as a Markov Decision Process (MDP) . An MDP is defined by a state space S, an action space A, a transition probability P px1|x, a q, a one stage cost function f px, a q, and a discount factor 

ő≥ P p 0, 1q. Given a policy ŌÄ that determines the choice of actions an P A based on the current value of the state xn P S, the goal is to minimize the expected discounted cumulative cost: 

JpŌÄq ‚Äú EŌÄ

¬ę 8√Ņ

> n‚Äú0

ő≥nf pXn, A nq

ňáňáňáX0 ‚Äú x0

ff 

, (5.1) where we use upper case characters to denote random variables and lower cases for specific values or realizations of these random variables. Thus typically xn will denote a realization or a sample of the random variable Xn describing the state as a random variable in S. So the description of the MDP stipulates that Xn`1 is a random variable in S with distribu-tion P p¬®| xn, a nq when Xn ‚Äú xn and An ‚Äú an, and its realizations xn`1 are sampled from 

P p¬®| xn, a nq. The objective JpŌÄq is analogous to the multi-agent reward maximization de-scribed in Chapter 1 (see for instance equation (1.1)), but adapted to our cost-minimization framework. A distinguishing feature of RL is its model-free approach. In many practical scenarios, the agent does not have access to the internal dynamics of the environment (the transition kernel P ). Instead, they learn by interacting with the environment, observing states, taking actions, and incurring costs without a prior model of the environment‚Äôs transition or cost structure. This paradigm shift requires the agent to balance the exploration of possibly new parts of the state and action spaces, with the exploitation of known low-cost regions, a challenge that lies at the heart of the algorithms discussed in this chapter. CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE FIRST ABSTRACT MODEL 90 

5.2.2 Value-based methods and Q-learning 

Value-based methods rely on the evaluation of the state-action value function, or Q-function, the value Qpx, a q representing the expected cumulative cost incurred by starting in a state 

x, taking an initial action a, and then following a policy ŌÄ thereafter: 

QŌÄpx, a q ‚Äú EŌÄ

¬ę 8√Ņ

> n‚Äú0

ő≥nf pXn, A nq

ňáňáňáX0 ‚Äú x, A 0 ‚Äú a

ff 

.

The optimal Q-function, denoted Qňö, satisfies the Bellman optimality equation: 

Qňöpx, a q ‚Äú f px, a q ` ő≥EX1‚ÄěP p¬®| x,a q

A fundamental value-based algorithm is Q-learning [151], which iteratively updates an esti-mate of Qňö using temporal difference learning. During training, upon observing a transition 

pxn, a n, c n, x n`1q, the Q-value is updated via:

where ő∑n is the learning rate. For environments with finite state and action spaces, these Q-values can be stored in a table, justifying the terminology tabular Q-learning . A critical aspect of such algorithms is the exploration-exploitation trade-off, often managed via an 

ŌĶ-greedy strategy, where the agent selects the action prescribed by the current policy with probability 1 ¬ī ŌĶ, and with probability ŌĶ, explores the action space by choosing an action uniformly at random. This tabular approach will be the foundation for the discretized simplex Mean Field Q-learning algorithm in Section 5.3.2. 

5.2.3 Policy gradient and zeroth-order optimization 

When dealing with continuous action spaces or highly parameterized policies (such as neural networks), value-based methods become computationally expensive due to the computation of the infimum inf a1 Qpx1, a 1q for each state x1. Policy gradient methods address this by directly parameterizing the policy ŌÄőłpxq with weights őł and performing stochastic gradient descent on the expected total cost Jpőłq.However, in many RL applications, calculating exact gradients is not possible because the environment acts as a black box. Zeroth-order optimization (also known as derivative-free optimization) overcomes this obstacle by estimating gradients using only cost evalu-ations. By applying small random perturbations u to the parameters and measuring the resulting cost Jpőł`uq, one can construct an unbiased estimate of the gradient of a smoothed objective function. This approach will be exploited in the case of LQ MFC models in Chap-ter 6. 

5.2.4 Actor-Critic and Deep RL 

To combine the benefits of value-based and policy-based methods, Actor-Critic architectures maintain two separate structures: the actor , representing the policy parameterized by őłŌÄ,CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE FIRST ABSTRACT MODEL 91 and the critic , estimating the value function parameterized by őłQ. The critic evaluates the current policy‚Äôs performance, providing a low-variance critique that the actor uses to guide its parameter updates. When state and action spaces are large or continuous, tabular methods are infeasible due to the curse of dimensionality. Deep RL solves this quandary by using artificial neural networks as function approximators for the actor and the critic. A prominent algorithm in this class is DDPG [111], an actor-critic method specifically designed for continuous action spaces. DDPG utilizes experience replay buffers and target networks to stabilize the neural network training. This methodology underpins the Mean Field DDPG algorithm presented in Section 5.3.3, enabling scalable numerical solutions applied to complex problems in Sec-tion 5.4. 

5.2.5 Mean Field MDP: notations and environment 

In the context of MFRL, we adapt the standard MDP paradigm to the lifted problem described in Chapter 3. We recall that the population distribution őľn P PpSq (where S is the individual state space) serves as the state of the MFMDP, often referred to as a level-1 state. The action , or level-1 action, is a joint distribution ¬Į an P PpS ňÜ Aq consistent with 

őľn, meaning its first marginal is őľn ‚Äú pr 1p¬Įanq. The environment in this setting is a mean field simulator that implements the lifted dynamics ¬ĮF : given the current distribution őľn

and the lifted action ¬Į an, it returns the next distribution őľn`1 and the aggregate social cost ¬Įf pőľn, ¬Įanq.Ideally, this environment provides a perfect simulation of the infinite-population dy-namics. However, in practice, simulating a continuous distribution őľn is often intractable, especially with high-dimensional state spaces. Consequently, numerical implementations frequently adopt a particle system simulator . In this approach, the environment simulates a system of N interacting agents Xn ‚Äú p X1

> n

, . . . , X Nn q and provides the empirical distribu-tion ňÜ őľNn ‚Äú 1

> N

ŇôNi‚Äú1 őīXin as an observation. Under suitable propagation-of-chaos assumptions, and conditionally on the common information when common noise is present, such empirical distributions approximate the corresponding mean-field distribution as N √Ď 8 .In the numerical examples of Section 5.4 in this chapter, we primarily focus on simu-lating the mean field dynamics directly. In contrast, Chapter 6 investigates more granular configurations. Specifically, Section 6.1.4 discusses an idealized McKean-Vlasov simulator, while Section 6.1.5 introduces a finite-population simulator. The latter allows for a theo-retical study and numerical illustration of the additional error induced by the size of the finite population. 

## 5.3 Mean Field Q-Learning 

5.3.1 Controls for finite state and action spaces 

In the rest of this section, we assume that S and A are finite, we denote their cardinal-ities by |S| and |A| respectively, and we denote by xp1q, . . . , x p| S|q and őĪp1q, . . . , őĪ p| A|q their elements. We first revisit the description of the action space and then propose two RL CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE FIRST ABSTRACT MODEL 92 methods for this setting. Subsequently, we explain in Section 5.3.3 how to adapt these RL techniques to continuous spaces. Before introducing the mean-field Q-learning algorithm, we first provide a representation of the set ¬Įőď ńé ¬ĮS ňÜ ¬ĮA ‚Äú PpSqňÜ PpS ňÜAq on which the ¬ĮQňö function is defined. Recall that the lifted state-action space ¬Įőď, defined in Equation (3.13) of Chapter 3, captures the requirement that the marginal of a lifted action must be consistent with the current mean field state. Since we assume that S is finite, its lifted space PpSq can be identified with a simplex 

S in R|S|. In other words, we treat a distribution őľ P PpSq as an |S|-dimensional vector 

pőľpiqqi‚Äú1,..., |S| whose non-negative coordinates sum up to one. Similarly, since A is finite, we identify PpAq with a simplex A in R|A|. However, representing admissible actions ¬Į a P ¬ĮU pőľq ńé 

PpS ňÜ Aq of the lifted MDP requires careful handling due to the underlying constraints. A first approach is to identify PpS ňÜ Aq with a simplex in R|S|ňÜ| A| and to view a lifted action ¬Įa as a |S| ňÜ | A| matrix `¬Įapxpiq, őĪ pjqqňė

In the remainder of this section, we propose two model-free algorithms based on the optimal state-action value function ¬ĮQňö : ¬Įőď √Ď R or, equivalently, ňúQňö : PpSq ňÜ ňúA √Ď R.

5.3.2 Simplex discretization and tabular MFQ-learning 

We consider two settings, depending on whether the controls at level-0 are mixed or pure. In both cases, we discretize the simplexes, which leads to a finite-state finite-action MDP for which the tabular Q-learning algorithm can be used. We establish its convergence. When using pure controls, we can prove the convergence of the value function as well as that of the associated optimal control. 

5.3.2.1 Q-learning with level-0 mixed controls 

We first consider the situation where the controls are mixed at the level-0, meaning that the generic agent can use randomized actions. Since the simplexes S and A are infinite, a tabular version of the Q-learning algorithm cannot be directly applied to approximate ňúQňö. To address this, we first approximate these simplexes using finite subsets ňáS ńā S and ňáA ńā A. Let ňáA ‚Äú t ňáa : S √Ď ňáAu. In particular, | ňáA| ‚Äú | ňáA||S| because we identify functions in ňáA with |S|-dimensional vectors whose entries take values in the finite set ňáA. To ensure that the mean-field term takes values in the finite set ňáS, we use a projection: At time n,given őľn P ňáS, we compute őľn`1 ‚Äú ¬ĮF pőľn, őľ n ňÜb ňáa, őĶ 0

> n`1

Precise definitions of the discretization and the projection are provided below, after introducing a discrete version of the original MFC problem. Here ňáQ : ňáS ňÜ ňáA √Ď R can be represented by a matrix (also called a table) in R| ňáS|ňÜ| ňáA| and is viewed as an approximation of ňúQňö : PpSq ňÜ ňúA √Ď R of the original MFC problem. This problem can be viewed as an MDP with finite state and action spaces. In this case, one can adapt in a straightforward way the classical tabular Q-learning algorithm. The pseudo-code is provided in Algorithm 1. Note that, even in the absence of common noise, this algorithm is possibly stochastic since at each episode, the order in which the state-action pairs are picked is potentially random. In practice, the order could be fixed in advance or stem from a sampled trajectory, which we use in the numerical examples provided in Section 5.4. 

Remark 5.1 . In this chapter, we adopt the perspective of a central planner who learns to control the distribution to minimize a social cost. For simplicity in the RL application, we assume the planner directly observes the mean-field population distribution. This allows us to analyze the convergence of an RL algorithm rigorously. However, in practice it is more likely that the planner could not observe this ideal distribution but rather a distorted version, such as the empirical distribution over a finite number of agents. In Section 6.1, we will present and prove the convergence of a model-free policy gradient algorithm for the LQ setting in the case where an empirical distribution is observed instead of the mean-field one. In the present situation, it is reasonable to expect that the propagation of chaos could be used to design and analyze a Q-learning algorithm based on the mere observation of a finite population, but this is beyond the scope of the present discussion. 

Remark 5.2 . In the finite state setting, the set of empirical distributions with N agents can be interpreted as the set of histograms where each bin can only take values that are multiples of 1 {N : tp őľxqxPS : @x P S, őľ x P t k{N, k ‚Äú 0, . . . , N u, Ňô

Remark 5.3 . In practice, the subsets ňáS and ňáA should be chosen based on two criteria. First, 

őĶS and őĶA should be as small as possible. Second, the number of points in these subsets, namely | ňáS| and | ňáA|, should be as small as possible. These quantities appear in the bound given by Theorem 5.1 below. Of course, to decrease őĶS, we expect that more points are needed and hence | ňáS| should increase (and likewise for őĶA and | ňáA|). This trade-off has to be managed carefully. A standard choice is to cover the probability simplex with balls of a given radius. If the dimension of the simplex is d and the radius of these balls is őī, then the number of points (centers of the balls) is asymptotically of order őė pőī1¬īdq. In our setting, the number of points would be of order őė pőĶ1¬ī| S|

Assumption 12 is similar to the covering time assumption in [59]. One way to satisfy this assumption is to fix an ordering of the state-action pairs and loop through it repeatedly. In this case,

In practice, it is common to take ňá an using a randomized policy, such as an ŌĶ-greedy policy, which amounts to taking action arg min ňáQnpňáőľn, ¬®q with probability 1 ¬ī ŌĶ, and otherwise to pick an action uniformly at random in ňáA. In standard Q-learning, this is interpreted as a randomized policy. In our mean-field context, this is a form of common randomization. The main advantage of this approach is that, as the Q-function is learned, the sequence of actions is mostly concentrated on the optimal choices instead of spending many iterations on irrelevant actions. However, when the Q-function is not yet well estimated, using an ŌĶ-greedy policy with a small ŌĶ can lead to poor decisions. This is an illustration of the exploration and exploitation trade-off. The proof of convergence of the Q-learning algorithm can be extended to stochastic state-action sequences, provided the corresponding covering time is bounded by a constant with high probability. For instance, it is enough to assume that the covering time is smaller than L with probability 1 {2. In practice, exploration of the state-action space is often guaranteed by a combination of action randomization and exploring starts at different episodes (provided the learner can query an oracle which simulates transitions from any pőľ, ňúaq). 5.3.2.2 Q-learning with pure level-0 controls 

The approach described above is designed for cases where we seek optimal actions that may be randomized at the individual level. Searching in the space PpAq comes with a computa-tional cost that is reflected in the bounds through the cardinality of the discrete simplex ňáA.However, in some situations it may be interesting to directly search for actions that are pure at the individual level. In this paragraph, we restrict our attention to pure level-0 policies, i.e., without idiosyncratic randomization at the agent level. For such policies, given the recommendation of the central planner (which might depend on a common randomization), each agent chooses their action in a deterministic way, based purely on their own state. This amounts to removing the random variables ŌĎn from the probabilistic framework presented in Section 3.2.1. For simplicity we will use the same notations for the value functions ¬ĮJňö,¬ĮQňö and ňáQ. The goal is now to approximate ¬ĮQňö, which is the Q-function optimized over the set of policies that are pure at the level 0. In this case, instead of (5.2), the set of strategy functions is (for simplicity we keep the notation ňúA): ňúA :‚Äú t ňúa : S √Ď Au ‚Äú AS .

In Algorithm 1, we replace ňá a P ňáA by ňú a P ňúA.

5.3.3 Deep reinforcement learning for MFMDP 

The tabular Q-learning method of the previous section is sufficiently straightforward to allow for a detailed analysis. However, it cannot be used in practice for large state or action spaces because of the computational cost of discretizing the simplexes. An alternative is to work directly with continuous spaces, in which case the policies and value functions cannot be represented in a tabular way. Instead, we can rely on function approximation. To this CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE FIRST ABSTRACT MODEL 104 end, we now propose methods from deep RL which are more suitable for continuous spaces. The motivations are twofold. First, if S and A are finite but we want to learn an optimal policy that is potentially randomized at level-0, the discretization approach proposed in ¬ß 5.3.2.1 has a complexity that increases with the number of points in the discretization of PpAq, which itself increases exponentially quickly with the cardinality of A. For this reason, it can be interesting to tackle directly PpAq as a continuous action space and to use deep RL methods for continuous action space MDPs. Second, some MFC problems are naturally posed with a continuous state space S. In this case, under mild conditions, the optimal policy is in fact non-randomized not only at the level-1 but even at the level-0 (see, e.g., [34, 19] and the references therein). However, the state of the MFMDP is an element of the infinite dimensional space PpSq. As noted in Chapter 3, this measure-valued state allows the central planner to treat the collective behavior of an infinite population as a single Markovian process on the simplex ¬ĮS ‚Äú PpSq.From a numerical viewpoint, we need two ingredients: (1) a finite-dimensional approxima-tion of the mean field and (2) a parameterized approximation of the value function or the policy taking this finite-dimensional representation of the mean field state as an input. For the second point, we will again use deep neural networks. For the first point, for the sake of definiteness, we choose to simply replace PpSq by Pp ňáSq where ňáS is a discretization of S with a finite number of points. We assume that, given ňá őľ P Pp ňáSq and a level-1 action ňá a : S √Ď A,one can get from the environment a sample of the next state and the associated cost ňúf pňáőľ, ňáaq.The problem thus reduces to an MDP with finite dimensional (but potentially continuous) state and action spaces. Such MDPs can be solved with a variety of deep RL algorithms. In the following, we provide numerical illustrations based on the DDPG proposed in [111]. It relies on two neural networks, one for the Q-function (the critic) and one for the policy (the actor). The core of the algorithm involves alternating updates: the critic is updated by min-imizing an empirical square error, while the actor is updated through gradient descent. To improve exploration, a Gaussian noise ŌĶan`1 is added to the action prescribed by the actor. Furthermore, for more stability, target networks are also added. We refer to [111] for more details. We write the algorithm below in cost-minimization form: the critic approximates cumulative cost and the actor is updated by descending the estimated cost. The algorithm is summarized in Algorithm 2. 

## 5.4 Numerical Examples 

In this section, we illustrate the above RL algorithms on two different examples. In each example, the representative agent‚Äôs state space and action space are finite. The first example is a cybersecurity model with 4 states, on which we illustrate both the tabular Q-learning algorithm and the DDPG algorithm. The second example features a larger number of states, leading us to apply only the DDPG algorithm, which offers the advantage of not requiring simplex discretization. 5.4.1 Example 1: Cybersecurity model 

For a first testbed, we start with a finite state problem. We revisit the cybersecurity example introduced in [99], now adopting the perspective of a central planner (such as a large company or a state) aiming to protect its system against hacker attacks. The situation can be phrased as an MFC problem. In this model, the population consists of a large group of computers which can be either defended (D) or undefended (U), and either infected (I) or susceptible (S) of infection. Hence the set S has four elements corresponding to the four possible combinations: DI, DS, UI, US. The action set is A ‚Äú t 0, 1u, where 0 is interpreted as the fact that the central planner is satisfied with the current protection level (D or U) of the computer under consideration, whereas 1 indicates a desire to modify this level. In the latter case, the update occurs at a (fixed) rate őĽ ńÖ 0. If the controls are pure at level-0, the central planner assigns a single action to each of the four states, which is then applied to all computers at that state. If the controls are mixed at level-0, the planner chooses a distribution over actions for each state, and each computer in that state then independently selects an action according to the chosen distribution. When infected, each computer may recover at rate 

qDrec or qUrec depending on whether it is defended or not. On the other hand, a computer may be infected either directly by a hacker, at rate vH qDinf (resp. vH qUinf ) if it is defended (resp. undefended), or by undefended infected computers, at rate ő≤U U őľpt U I uq (resp. ő≤U D őľpt U I uq )if it is undefended (resp. defended), or by defended infected computers, at rate ő≤DU őľpt DI uq 

(resp. ő≤DD őľpt DI uq ) if it is undefended (resp. defended). Here vH can be interpreted as the attack intensity parameter. In short, the infinitesimal generator is given by:

At each time step, the central planner pays a protection cost kD ńÖ 0 for each defended computer, and a penalty kI ńÖ 0for each infected computer. The instantaneous cost in the MFMDP is thus defined as: ¬Įf pőľ, ¬Įaq ‚Äú kDőľpt DI, DS uq ` kI őľpt DI, U I uq , pőľ, ¬Įaq P ¬ĮS ňÜ ¬ĮA. 

The optimal control and optimal flow of distributions can be characterized by a forward-backward ODE system which can be obtained in a way similar to what is done in the MFG setting e.g. in [34, ¬ß 7.2.3]. We will use this solution as a benchmark.

5.4.2 Example 2: Discrete distribution planning 

We now apply the MFC framework to a problem where the objective is to match a target distribution. We consider a model with parameters summarized in Table 5.2, where the agents‚Äô actions correspond to moving left, staying, or moving right. At the boundaries, attempted moves outside the grid are folded back to the boundary. The dynamics follow 

F px, a, őľ, e, e 0q ‚Äú ő†X pő†X px ` aq ` e0q. Here ő† X denotes projection onto the finite grid

Without common noise, the trajectories are deterministic and the final state distribution is very close to the target distribution. With common noise, the trajectories are stochastic and the final state distribution is slightly different from the target distribution. But we observe that in both scenarios, the DDPG agent successfully learns to move the population towards the target distribution. At the initial time, the control predominantly favors movement towards the center of the target distribution, while at the terminal time, the ‚ÄĚstay‚ÄĚ action becomes dominant as the target has been reached. Figure 5.11 shows the training progress in terms of the social cost for both cases. 

## 5.5 Notes and Complements 

RL has been applied to solve MFC and MFMDPs using Q-learning as presented in this chapter in [40], as well as in [79, 77]; see also [78]. The deep RL approach presented in this chapter was first introduced in [40]. Extensions include applications to mean-field type games, namely, Nash equilibria between a finite number of central planners solving MFC problems: [137] studied convergence of Nash Q-learning and application of DDPG to such problems, and an adaptation of the PPO algorithm to MFTGs has been proposed in [92]. Other algorithms include a two-timescale method introduced in [7] and whose con-vergence has been established in [9]. This approach has then been extended to study actor-critic methods for MFC in [9]. Alternative actor-critic methods have been proposed in [64, 128]. [17] proposed a learning algorithm based on linear function approximation. [116] proposed a method based on an adaptation of the classical policy gradient method. In the line of continuous-time RL developed by [148], [152] studied q-learning for MFC in continuous time. For model-free continuous-time mean-field control from discrete-time transition data, see also [16]. RL methods for partially observable MFC models has been proposed in [48, 49]. Related deep-learning methods for MFC with common noise, based on population-dependent controls and neural approximations of the mean field, have been studied in [52]. The model for cybersecurity considered in this chapter was introduced, in the context of CHAPTER 5. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE FIRST ABSTRACT MODEL 114 2 4 6 8 10

# Implementation of the LQ Mean Field Model 

Abstract. This chapter establishes global convergence results for policy-gradient methods in the Linear-Quadratic Mean Field Control setting introduced in Chapter 4. We analyze three configurations: model-based exact gradients, model-free gradients with an idealized McKean-Vlasov simulator, and model-free gradients with a finite-population simulator. We quantify how a finite collection of agents can learn a policy that is approx-imately optimal for the infinite-population problem. Numerical experiments illustrate the behavior of these methods under stochastic dynamics and finite-population simulation. 

Following the general numerical methods discussed in Chapter 5, this chapter focuses on the Linear-Quadratic Mean Field Control (LQMFC) setting. As introduced in Chap-ter 4, the family of Linear-Quadratic (LQ) models is central to optimal control due to its mathematical tractability and wide-ranging applicability. In reinforcement learning (RL), these models serve as an important benchmark for analyzing the convergence of iterative algorithms [61, 133]. Mean-field type control, or McKean-Vlasov control, extends the LQ framework by in-corporating state and action distributions. While Chapter 5 explored tabular and deep RL for general MFMDPs, the specific structure of LQ models allows us to establish global convergence guarantees for Policy Gradient (PG) methods, even in model-free settings. Our analysis generalizes the results of Fazel et al. [61] to the mean-field case, proving that the optimal parameters of the feedback control can be effectively learned. A key feature of our model is the presence of common noise , which captures random shocks affecting all agents simultaneously (see Chapter 4). While it complicates the analysis, it also makes the limiting population distribution stochastic. In our model-free investiga-tions, we study two types of simulators: an idealized McKean‚ÄďVlasov (MKV) simulator in 

¬ß 6.1.4 and a more realistic finite-population simulator in ¬ß 6.1.5. Proving convergence of the latter relies on the propagation of chaos. Numerical experiments illustrate these methods in ¬ß 6.5. 117 CHAPTER 6. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE LQ MEAN FIELD MODEL 118

Remark 6.1 . In the absence of noise processes, if the initial perturbations pőĶ0, őĶ 00q are non-degenerate with bounded supports (implying Assumption 15), the arguments in [61] can be applied to processes Y and Z, respectively, to show the convergence of the PG algorithm using the gradients p‚ąáK Cpőłq, ‚ąáLCpőłqq . In the more general setting considered here, par-ticularly with non-degenerate sub-Gaussian distributions, the strict positivity of őĽ1 

> y

and őĽ0

> z

allows us to derive convergence results without enforcing assumptions on the initial states as in previous work [61]. It is important to emphasize that the presence of noise terms poses additional difficulties in proving the convergence of PG algorithms in model-free scenarios. The challenges arise not only from the unbounded noise process but also from the quadratic forms involving sub-Gaussian random vectors Yn or Zn whose entries are not independent. We cannot simply rely on the classical matrix Bernstein inequality or the Hanson-Wright inequality [146] for independent random variables; thus, we require more delicate arguments to demonstrate convergence. 6.1.4 Model-free PG for MFC with MKV simulator 

We now turn to a modification of the PG updates (6.14), in which the gradient will be approximated in a model-free way. The new update scheme takes the form of PG updates with MKV simulator : #

Kpk`1q ‚Äú Kpkq ¬ī ő∑ ňú‚ąáT,M,ŌĄ K pőłpkqq,Lpk`1q ‚Äú Lpkq ¬ī ő∑ ňú‚ąáT,M,ŌĄ L pőłpkqq, (6.21) where ňú‚ąáT,M,ŌĄ pőłq ‚Äú ` ňú‚ąáT,M,ŌĄ K pőłq, ňú‚ąáT,M,ŌĄ L pőłqňė is defined below, based on samples generated by a simulator. Let us assume that we have access to the following (stochastic) simulator, called MKV simulator STM KV : given an admissible control parameter őł P őė, STM KV pőłq returns a sample of the discounted MF cost (see Section 4.2.2 and equation (4.7)) for the MKV dynamics (4.6) controlled by the process paőłnqnńõ0 and truncated at a finite horizon T . Without full knowledge of the model, we use derivative-free methods (see Section 6.3) to estimate the cost gradient. The main idea is to evaluate the function to minimize on a set of perturbed parameters so that one can move towards a weighted average direction that improves the value of the target function [120]. It has been shown that a careful implementation of ES can provide a performance improvement compared with classical RL algorithms based on back-propagation. Moreover, the method can be easily scaled up and adapted to distributed computer systems [135, 45]. We define some notation for the gradient estimation. 6.1.5 Model-free PG for MFC with population simulator 

We now turn our attention to a more realistic setting where one does not have access to an oracle for simulating the MKV dynamics, but only to an oracle capable of simulating the evolution of N agents. We then use the state sample average instead of the theoretical conditional mean, and for the social cost, the empirical average instead of the mean-field cost provided by the MKV simulator. We rely on the following population simulator ST,N pop : given a control parameter őł,

ST,N pop pőłq returns a sample of the social cost obtained by generating realizations of the N -agent state trajectories controlled by őł and computing the associated cost for the population, see (4.1) and (4.5). In other words, it returns a realization of ňúCT,N pőłq ‚Äú

Let CN pőłq ‚Äú JN pUőłq in equation (4.4) when all agents adopt the same control parameter őł P őė. Notice that this population simulator ST,N pop is arguably more realistic, though less power-ful, than the previous MKV simulator STM KV . The former uses only a noisy approximation of the true mean processes, while the MKV simulator generates the exact means. We em-phasize that, in this population simulator, all agents adopt the same control and therefore use the same perturbed version of the control parameter in Algorithm 4. This design aligns with the idea that the problem corresponds to an optimization problem for a central planner or a group of cooperative agents using the same control rule to minimize the social cost. For the one-step gradient update scheme with population simulator ST,N pop , we replace the gradient ňú‚ąáT,M,ŌĄ pőłq obtained from Algorithm 3 with an MKV simulator by another zero-th CHAPTER 6. ## 6.2 Technical results in Section 6.1.3 

This section collects the technical lemmas used for proving the statements in Section 6.1.3. These lemmas are adapted from [61] with necessary modifications to cope with the idiosyn-cratic noise and the common noise processes present in the state dynamics. To make the paper self-contained, we provide their proofs here. Because there is an analogy between the processes Y and Z in their definitions and usages, we will state and prove results related to the process Y and the parameter K only.

## 6.3 Technical lemma for Section 6.1.4 

6.3.1 Intuition for zeroth-order optimization 

Derivative-free optimization (see e.g. [46, 120]) tries to find an optimizer of a function 

f : x √ě √Ď f pxq using only pointwise values of f , without having access to its gradient. The basic idea is to express the gradient of f in a point x by using values of f p¬®q at a Gaussian perturbation region around point x. This can be achieved by introducing an approximation of f using a Gaussian smoothing. Formally, if we define the perturbed function for some 

ŌÉ ńÖ 0 as fŌÉ2 pxq ‚Äú Erf px ` őĶqs , where őĶ ‚Äě N p0, ŌÉ 2Iq, then its gradient can be written as CHAPTER 6. IMPLEMENTATION OF THE LQ MEAN FIELD MODEL 142

Remark 6.6 . We note that the sample complexity bounds, such as Ōēsample,size above and the truncation horizon Ōētruncation , are highly sensitive to the discount factor ő≥. In particular, as ő≥ √Ď 1, the terms p1 ¬ī ?ő≥q2 and p1 ¬ī ő≥pert q2 in the denominators cause these bounds to grow significantly. This reflects a known challenge in infinite-horizon reinforcement learning, where the effective horizon diverges as the discount factor approaches 1.

## 6.5 Numerical Results 

In this section, we provide numerical illustrations of the RL algorithms developed for the LQMFC problem. We compare the performance of the exact PG (model-based) with the model-free approach using both the MKV simulator and the finite-population simulator. The code is available at: https://github.com/mlauriere/mfrl-tutorial-code .

6.5.1 Experimental Setup 

We consider a scalar LQMFC model where the dynamics and costs are symmetric across the agent population. The representative agent‚Äôs objective is to minimize the socially optimal cost by learning the parameters őł ‚Äú p K, L q, which determine the decentralized control 

an ‚Äú ¬ī KpXn ¬ī ¬ĮXnq ¬ī L ¬ĮXn.The model parameters and hyperparameters used for the simulation are summarized in Table 6.1. The dynamics are characterized by coefficients A, ¬ĮA, B, ¬ĮB, and the cost function by Q, ¬ĮQ, R, ¬ĮR. The noise in the dynamics is modeled as zero-mean Gaussian with variances 

ŌÉ2 

> ŌĶ

We note that for the numerical illustrations, we use the Adam optimizer to adapt the learning rate during training. While the theoretical analysis presented in the previous sections relies on a fixed learning rate for simplicity and to derive clear convergence rates, Adam is employed here for its practical efficiency and superior convergence properties in stochastic environments. This choice serves to illustrate the robust performance of the developed algorithms in a more standard machine learning implementation. We study numerically the three primary configurations analyzed above: 1. Exact PG : Uses model-based exact gradients as a baseline. 2. Adam MF-MKV : Model-free PG using the idealized MKV simulator. 3. Adam MF-Pop ( N ): Model-free policy gradient using the finite-population simula-tor with N agents. We compare N ‚Äú 1 and N ‚Äú 10 to study the impact of population size on the estimation error. 

6.5.2 Discussion of Results 

The results are aggregated over several independent seeds (see Table 6.1) and smoothed using a running average. In the figures below, the solid lines represent the mean value across seeds, while the shaded areas represent the standard deviation. Figure 6.1 illustrates the convergence of the social cost. The left plot shows the cumu-lative cost for each method approaching the theoretical optimal value (the black dashed line). The right plot displays the logarithm of the distance to the optimal cost, highlighting the efficiency of the gradient estimation. We observe that while the MKV simulator closely tracks the exact gradient performance, the population simulator exhibits higher variance and a larger gap, which decreases significantly as N increases from 1 to 10. ## 6.6 Notes and Complements 

The convergence proof in this chapter builds upon the approach proposed by [61] for policy-gradient methods in classical LQR, and extends it by including stochastic dynamics and mean-field interactions. PG methods have also been applied to linear-quadratic mean field type games, i.e., non-cooperative games between central planners solving MFC problems, see [36, 37, 163]. Further related work on LQ mean field learning includes [150, 65, 53]. Other RL algorithms for LQ MFC problems include the two-timescale method of [7] and the policy iteration method studied in [109]. [155, 154] proposed an approach based on integral RL for the case of multiplicative noise and robust mean field social control. Bibliography
